Hannon's Take: At The Eye of the Storm

Fed's Fischer Expresses Caution About Global Housing Markets

Fed's Dudley Sees More Tightening Ahead, Economy on Even Keel'

Evans Says Fed Likely Needs Time to Decide Next Rate Move

Bank of England Governor Sees Weak Wage Growth Delaying Rate Rises

At The Eye of the Storm

Last Thursday evening, Bank of England Gov. Mark Carney was scheduled to deliver his annual "Mansion House speech" to the grandees of the City of London.

But he did not. Instead, the dinner was canceled out of respect for the people left dead or homeless by the Grenfell Tower fire.

Mr. Carney eventually delivered the speech at a breakfast Tuesday morning. But it was the second straight year in which the event has been delayed in response to a national tragedy: In 2016 it was because of the murder of Jo Cox [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-k-lawmaker-injured-after-incident-in-parliamentary-constituency-1466085309], a lawmaker from the opposition Labour Party.

The repeated disruptions to Mr. Carney's schedule reflect the turbulent times in which the central bank is working.

Its ambitions to normalize monetary policy and raise its key interest rate last year were frustrated by the U.K. vote to leave the European Union and the markets' response.

This year, the policy outlook remains clouded by developments beyond its control. The surprise outcome of the June election suggests fiscal policy is likely to be a little or a lot looser than once seemed likely, with unclear implications for rate setters.

Then there are the Brexit negotiations, which began Monday. Almost a year after the vote, the central bank has little more clarity about the terms under which that will take place. Policy makers are assuming a "smooth" exit, or one governed by an agreement rather than none at all. That seems the most likely outcome, but British politics is suddenly full of surprises.

For now, policy makers are doing the only thing they can, which is to try to understand why inflation is above their 2% target and how long it is likely to be so. They face some difficult decisions in a combustible environment.

Should they let inflation run much higher, a tightening squeeze on incomes could fuel the already strong sense of popular resentment against the establishment. But a series of interest rate increases might go down just as badly.

But there may be a crumb of comfort. In what are abnormal and unsettling times, Britons may find it reassuring were central bankers to do something that they are supposed to do: raise interest rates when inflation threatens to get out of control.

Key Developments Around the World

Fed's Fischer Expresses Caution About Global Housing Markets

By Harriet Torry

Federal Reserve Vice Chairman Stanley Fischer urged global policy makers to continue efforts to strengthen the resilience of their housing markets, echoing earlier cautionary words from Fed officials about the potential risks of having low interest rates over long periods.

"House prices are now high and rising in several countries, perhaps as a result of extended periods of low interest rates," Mr. Fischer said in prepared remarks released ahead of delivery Tuesday at a closed-door conference on macroprudential policy hosted by the Dutch and Swedish central banks in Amsterdam.

While Mr. Fischer said the financial system is stronger than before the recession that ended in 2009, "there is more to be done, and much improvement to be preserved and built on, for the world as we know it cannot afford another pair of crises of the magnitude of the Great Recession and the global financial crisis."

The Fed's No. 2 official highlighted the need for a well-capitalized banking system in promoting financial stability, as well as stress tests that examine scenarios with sharp declines in house prices. Mr. Fischer didn't comment on the immediate outlook for U.S. monetary policy in his comments.

Other Fed officials have spoken out about the threat of asset price bubbles emerging due to years of low interest rates. Boston Fed chief Eric Rosengren has long worried about potential bubbles in the hot commercial real-estate market. Minneapolis Fed President Neel Kashkari said in May that taking steps to make the financial sector more resilient is perhaps the best way to deal with asset price bubbles.

Mr. Fischer said macroprudential policies that focus on borrowers, lenders and securitization may be useful in strengthening financial stability, but "even though macroprudential policies reduce the incidence and severity of housing related crises, they may still occur."

He also said that "a well-capitalized banking system is a necessary condition for stability in bank-based financial systems as well as those with large nonbank sectors."

Fed's Dudley Sees More Tightening Ahead, Economy on Even Keel'

By David Harrison

PLATTSBURGH, N.Y.Federal Reserve Bank of New York President William Dudley said Monday a gradual pace of rate increases will help prolong the economic recovery. In remarks to a business group in upstate New York, Mr. Dudley signaled a willingness to continue the process of slowly raising interest rates as a way to keep the economy humming.

"If we were not to withdraw accommodation, the risk would be that the economy would crash to a very, very low unemployment rate and generate inflation," he said.

That would create the risk that "we would need to slam on the brakes and generate a recession," he said. "We very much want to avoid that."

Mr. Dudley said the Fed was trying to strike a balance between generating economic growth without at the same time causing inflation to rise too rapidly. "The idea is to have a sustainable expansion that lasts a very long time," he said. "To do that we need to keep the economy on an even keel."

"So far, we've gotten the balance roughly right," he added.

The Fed last week raised its benchmark interest rate by a quarter percentage point to a range of between 1% and 1.25%.

After raising rates only once in both 2015 and 2016, the central bank now has put in place two rate increases in 2017. Officials also have penciled in one further rate increase this year as well as three for 2018. But some economists say sluggish inflation growth could force them to rethink their plans.

Mr. Dudley said he was optimistic about the economy and expected inflation to gradually move up. "If the labor market continues to tighten, wages will gradually pick up, and with that we'll see inflation get back to 2%," he said.

The New York Fed chief added he wasn't concerned about the fact that Treasury yields have been falling despite the Fed's recent tightening moves. "We haven't tightened financial conditions to a significant degree," he said. "The economy hasn't been restrained very much."

"I don't take a lot of signal from the fact that the 10-year yield is low," he said. Compared with government bond yields in Japan and Europe, U.S. yields "are actually pretty high," he said.

Central banks in Japan and Europe have dropped benchmark interest rates below zero and are buying bonds to stimulate their economies. The Fed, by contrast, plans this year to begin unwinding its portfolio of assets purchased in the aftermath of the financial crisis.

Evans Says Fed Likely Needs Time to Decide Next Rate Move

By Michael S. Derby

Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago President Charles Evans expressed support on Monday for the U.S. central bank's plan to raise rates further, but added he believes officials can take some time before acting again to ensure inflation is truly moving higher.

Interest rate rises so far have been "reasonable," Mr. Evans told reporters after a speech before a gathering of the New York University Money Marketeers group. But he added "I don't think we would be poorly served by allowing more time to pass and take in the data" before boosting short-term rates again.

Mr. Evans, a voting member of the interest-rate setting Federal Open Market Committee, was offering his first public comments since last week's gathering of the committee.

Then, officials lifted their short-term interest rate target range for the second time during the year, to 1% and 1.25%. They also said they see about one more rate rise coming over the rest of the year, in forecasts that predicted continued improvement in the labor market and a slower path back to the official 2% inflation target.

The Fed's rate outlook was puzzling to some in light of recent data showing inflation trends are moving lower, rather than higher, as officials had expected. Some Fed officials have said if the pace of inflation gains doesn't accelerate it could call into question the need for rate rises later this year.

"The current environment supports very gradual rate hikes and slow preset reductions in our balance sheet," Mr. Evans said in his speech. He said he views "slow removal of accommodation as necessary support to symmetrically achieving our 2% inflation goal in a timely fashion."

Mr. Evans declined to give a personal view on how many rate rises he believes are warranted this year. "Much ink will be spilled on the importance of two, versus three, versus four rate increases this year and subplots regarding our balance sheet policy," he said, adding, "I don't want to get hung up over small differences."

Mr. Evans acknowledged the Fed's inflation dilemma and he said he expects it to take longer to get inflation back to 2% relative to his colleagues. What's more, he said he sees "downside risks" to the inflation outlook, and added "we need to demonstrate a strong commitment to hitting our symmetric inflation objective sooner rather than later."

Mr. Evans noted that there is a lot of uncertainty around the factors that create inflation. He said "there is a host of experience" that suggest that when the job market gets hot, inflation follows.

Mr. Evans said given what's happened with price pressures, increases in short-term rates haven't served to offer much restraint to either the economy or rising price pressures. But from here "it becomes a more difficult discussion" for central bankers.

Some of the Fed's inflation problem may be self induced. Noting the Fed's price rise target isn't a ceiling but one where prices pressures below and above the goal are equally unwelcome, he said such a regime "can be a tough policy objective for conservative central bankers to deliver on."

And when the public sees the Fed repeatedly fail to achieve its target, its own expectations could soften, believing inflation will always stay under the target, Mr. Evans said. That will in turn make it even harder to get inflation higher.

In his speech, Mr. Evans was upbeat about other parts of the economy's performance. "The real economy is performing well, and we essentially have met our employment mandate," he said.

Mr. Evans also reiterated that he believes changes in the economy mean the Fed will probably not be able to lift its interest rate target as high as it has in the past, and that means the risk of falling back to near zero rates will be higher.

More Banks Target September for Fed's Balance-Sheet Announcement

By Katy Burne

A handful of banks have moved up their forecasts for when the Federal Reserve will announce intentions to start its balance-sheet reduction to September from December, following Chairwoman Janet Yellen's remarks last week that the central bank could begin the process "relatively soon."

Some analysts took Ms. Yellen's comments to mean that the Fed is fine-tuning the timing of its unwinding, ahead of a third interest-rate increase it has penciled in for the year.

In a May survey by the New York Fed, respondents on average placed a 40% probability on the Fed announcing a change in its $4.5 trillion portfolio in the fourth quarter of this year, and a 17% likelihood for the third quarter.

"It seems like they want to prioritize the balance sheet," said Mark Cabana, interest-rate strategist at Bank of America Merrill Lynch and a former Fed official. "Rate hikes may be secondary to this next step."

Before Ms. Yellen's comments, Bank of America predicted that the Fed would raise rates in September and announce changes to its bond portfolio in December. J.P. Morgan also predicted a rate rise in September and the balance-sheet announcement in December. Michael Feroli, chief U.S. economist at J.P. Morgan, said the bank switched its view given Ms. Yellen's "relatively soon" comment.

Before its latest policy meeting, the Fed had signaled in March and May minutes it likely would be appropriate to begin shrinking the portfolio later this year. Fed governor Lael Brainard said in May the time for a change in balance-sheet policy was "coming into clearer view."

The narrowing timeline comes as bond-market dynamics that made the Fed nervous in previous tightening cycles have re-emerged. When yields on longer bonds in the U.S. Treasury market don't rise faster than yields on short-term bonds, the pattern is called a flattening yield curve. That breakdown in the usual upward slope from short- to long-term rates is widely watched because economists say that a severe flattening, or an inversion, can signal a worsening economy.

Today's yield curve has an upward slope but is much flatter than some had expected at this point in the cycle. The gap between the yields on the two-year and 10-year Treasury notes was 0.81 percentage point Monday, close to its narrowest point since 2007 and down from 0.92 percentage point a few weeks ago.

Rising long-term Treasury prices are partly a byproduct of the Fed's careful communication over its balance-sheet plans, and could be a potential problem down the road.

On the one hand, the Fed wants to foster stable economic conditions, and has delivered messages to help ensure investors avoid dumping bonds. On the other hand, the rally in bonds has ensured that low rates have persisted on everything from mortgages to car loans, whose rates key off the 10-year Treasury note, proving a further windfall for consumers even as the Fed seeks to make sure the economy doesn't overheat.

Minutes to their May policy meeting showed Fed officials discussing the decline in long-term Treasury rates last month and acknowledged that recent moves to lift their federal-funds rate "had not resulted in a tightening of financial conditions."

Former Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan faced an unusual yield curve when trying to tighten financial conditions in 2004, calling the distortions a "conundrum."

Some analysts think the flattening yield curve is encouraging the Fed to address its bond portfolio sooner than later to prevent further flattening. Letting more maturing bonds roll off its portfolio instead of reinvesting in bond markets would increase available supply, causing the yields of longer- term bonds to rise as the Fed raises short-term rates.

Credit Suisse analysts think the Fed's changes will increase net supply of Treasurys by 30% over the first three years, leading to a roughly 0.25 percentage point increase in rates on the 10-year Treasury note.

"The curve is flattening from both ends," because the Fed is raising short-term rates while rates on longer-term bonds are falling, but "the Fed won't let the yield curve invert," said Steven Ricchiuto, chief economist at Mizuho Securities USA Inc.

Sen. Warren Calls for Removal of 12 Wells Fargo Directors

By Emily Glazer

Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D, Mass.) is urging the Federal Reserve to remove a dozen Wells Fargo board directors who served during the bank's sales-practices scandal, according to a letter reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

In the letter sent to Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen on Monday, Sen. Warren urged the Fed to invoke its authority under a rule that allows it to remove certain people associated with depository institutions under specific circumstances.

Sen. Warren pointed to 12 directors who served on the San Francisco bank's board between May 2011 and July 2015, a period in which Wells Fargo fired 5,300 employees for the bad behavior. The bank settled with two regulators and a city official in September for $185 million over the practices. Its chief executive later abruptly retired and the bank continues to face state and federal investigations, which it has said it is cooperating with.

A Wells Fargo spokeswoman said the board and its management team have "taken many actions in response to its retail sales practices issues, including changes in senior leadership, executive accountability actions and numerous steps to ensure we make things right with any customer affected by unacceptable sales practices." She added that the work is ongoing.

The bank's board has also endured harsh criticism and faced opposition during a contentious shareholder meeting in late April. Several directors were re-elected to their roles by just a slim margin.

Sen. Warren has been a sharp critic of Wells Fargo's management and handling of the sales-practices scandal. When former CEO John Stumpf appeared before the Senate Banking Committee to explain the problems, Sen. Warren lambasted him and said he should resign and be criminally investigated.

In her eight-page letter to the Fed, Sen. Warren wrote that the sales-practices scandal "revealed severe problems with the bank's risk management practicesproblems that justify the Federal Reserve's removal of all responsible Board members."

In the wake of the scandal, Wells Fargo's board conducted an investigation of the bank, but largely didn't fault directors. On a call with media after the report was disclosed in April, Chairman Stephen Sanger said "the findings of the investigation showed the board took the appropriate actions with the information it had, when it had it" and that it hired a law firm to do the investigation to get "an independent and objective assessment of our performance."

Sen. Warren's letter cited the Fed's ability to remove board members including if they "engaged or participated in any unsafe or unsound practice" that caused certain depository institutions to "suffer financial loss" and that demonstrated "continuing disregardfor the safety and soundness" of the institution.

"I urge the Federal Reserve to use the tools Congress has given it to remove the responsible Board members and protect the continued safety and soundness of one of the country's largest banks," the senator's letter said.

The 12 board directors mentioned in the letter are: John D. Baker II, John S. Chen, Lloyd H. Dean, Elizabeth A. Duke, Enrique Hernandez, Jr., Donald M. James, Cynthia H. Milligan, Federico F. Pena, James H. Quigley, Stephen W. Sanger, Susan G. Swenson, and Suzanne M. Vautrinot.

Among those directors, Ms. Duke, who is now the board's vice chair, is a former Fed governor.

Sen. Warren wrote to the Fed that the board "failed to create an adequate risk management framework that would have alerted it to systemic problems with retail sales practices. It also caused long-lasting reputational damage to the bank that has eroded the bank's customer base."

The board's own report about the sales scandal, according to the letter, showed its "continuing disregard" for the bank's safety and soundness and failure to seriously address the sales-practices issues despite years of concerns.

The board's April report largely placed blame on Mr. Stumpf and former retail-banking head Carrie Tolstedt. The board clawed back an additional $75 million in compensation from them both.

The company was slow to stamp out the issues, the report said, in part because its decentralized structure gave Ms. Tolstedt too much power and officials from other units, such as risk management, human resources and legal, either couldn't connect the dots on problems or weren't powerful enough to do anything about it.

In late 2015, board members, including then-lead independent director Mr. Sanger, pushed Mr. Stumpf to remove Ms. Tolstedt from her role. But Mr. Stumpf declined, saying that Ms. Tolstedt, while at times controlling, was "the best banker in America," the report said.

The report also highlighted how the bank's push to boost revenue and profit trickled down to thousands of employees who felt pressured to meet unrealistic sales goals.

Bank of England Governor Sees Weak Wage Growth Delaying Rate Rises

By Paul Hannon

Bank of England Gov. Mark Carney on Tuesday said that while rate setters' tolerance for above-target inflation is coming to an end, it is too early to raise the key interest rate for the first time in a decade.

In a rescheduled speech to bankers at Mansion House in London, Mr. Carney said weak wage growth raised questions about the strength of domestic inflationary pressures, and he was unsure how the economy would respond to talks between the U.K. government and the rest of European Union on the terms of their separation.

"From my perspective, given the mixed signals on consumer spending and business investment, and given the still subdued domestic inflationary pressures, in particular anemic wage growth, now is not yet the time to begin that adjustment," he said.

After Mr. Carney's comments the pound fell 0.5% to $1.2674.

The pound's depreciation since the June 2016 vote to leave the EU has pushed up import prices and inflation. At its meeting earlier this month, the BOE's Monetary Policy Committee voted to leave the key interest rate unchanged at a record low of 0.25%.

However, three members dissented in favor of raising the Bank rate, the largest vote for such a move since the central bank last tightened policy in July 2007.

"Different members of the MPC will understandably have different views about the outlook and therefore on the potential timing of any Bank rate increase," Mr. Carney said. "But all expect that any changes would be limited in scope and gradual in pace."

Mr. Carney voted to leave the rate unchanged, but his comments suggest he views an increase as inevitable, although possibly some months away.

"Arguing to wait and see how the economy will react to the Brexit negotiationswhich have only just begun and will last at least two yearssuggests that Carney may be prepared to delay raising rates for a long time," said Allan Monks, an economist at J.P. Morgan.

Mr. Carney had been scheduled to deliver his speech Thursday, but the event was postponed as a show of respect to those who died or were left homeless in the Grenfell Tower fire.

That tragedy came amid a series of four terror attacks in as many months, and just a year after the murder of lawmaker Jo Cox led to the rescheduling of Mr. Carney's previous Mansion House speech.

"The best tribute this city and this country can give to the memories of those lost is to renew our shared commitmentwhatever our differencesto promote the common good," he said. "This includes pursuing a Brexit, and building an economy, that works for all."

In his speech, Mr. Carney also addressed the problem of large trade imbalances, which he said were now "politically as well as economically unsustainable."

His proposed solution is a drive by the Group of 20 largest economies to allow trade in services to be as free as trade in goods. This, he said, would remove an impediment to economies that are more dependent on services exportssuch as the U.S. and U.K.and allow them to narrow their trade gaps with countries that are more reliant on goods exports, such as Germany and China.

"The G-20 faces a choicebetween leveling down by putting more restrictions on goods trade, or leveling up by liberalizing trade in services," he said.

Mr. Carney estimated that "leveling up" would reduce the "excess deficits" of the U.S. by one third and the U.K. by one half.

Speaking before Mr. Carney at the Mansion House event, U.K. Treasury chief Philip Hammond echoed those sentiments.

"For the U.K. to be able to share fairly in the benefits of globalization, we need to lead a global crusade for liberalization of services," he said. "And we must employ that logic in our Brexit negotiations, to agree a bold and ambitious free-trade agreement with our EU counterparts that covers both goods and services."

The central banker identified the treatment of financial services in the Brexit talks, which started Monday, as an opportunity to put this new approach to trade into operation.

He also warned against fragmenting Europe's clearing market, responding to a plan proposed by the EU's executive arm last week that would subject clearinghouses operating outside the bloc to tougher oversight, including on-site inspections and access to financial accounts.

"Fragmentation is in no one's economic interest," he said. "Nor is it necessary for financial stability. Indeed it can damage it."

Silvana Tenreyro to Join BOE Rate-Setting Panel

By Paul Hannon and Wiktor Szary

LONDONProfessor Silvana Tenreyro of the London School of Economics and Political Science has been appointed to the Bank of England's Monetary Policy Committee, replacing Kristin Forbes, whose term ends in June.

Ms. Tenreyro will join the committee as an external member for a three-year term beginning July 7, the Bank of England said Monday.

The appointment comes at a challenging time for the central bank, with inflation set to exceed its target over coming years as the pound's weakening in the wake of a 2016 vote to leave the European Union stokes inflation.

"I am delighted to welcome Silvana Tenreyro to the Monetary Policy Committee and am very much looking forward to working with her," Gov. Mark Carney said.

"Her extensive and varied academic experienceon the monetary transmission mechanism, the dynamics of productivity, trade, housing issues as well as wage dynamics, to name just a fewwill be invaluable to the committee," he added.

In one recent paper, Ms. Tenreyro found evidence that monetary policy was more potent during economic expansions than contractions.

She has made few comments on BOE policy. However, she does have experience as a rate setter, sitting on the Bank of Mauritius's MPC from 2012 to 2014.

While there, she voted for rate cuts on three occasionsin line with the majority of other rate setters on two of those times. Otherwise, she voted for rates to be left unchanged, on some occasions against a minority that wanted to raise rates.

Over recent months, Ms. Forbes has been the most forceful advocate for a rise in interest rates at the BOE, and is regarded by analysts who follow the central bank as its leading "hawk." She was one of three members of the MPC to vote for a rise in the U.K.'s key interest rate this June, after annual inflation accelerated to 2.9% in May. This was well in excess of the BOE's 2% goal and the central bank's own forecasts.

To some analysts, Ms. Forbes successor appears to show signs of what is known as "dovishness," or a predisposition to keep interest rates low.

"Tenreyro's dovish voting behavior at the Bank of Mauritius is interesting as it could point to a dovish bias of the new external MPC member, in stark contrast to her predecessor Kristin Forbes," economists at Citigroup said in a note to clients. "However, that was a different MPC, a different time and a different country, making us very cautious in drawing any inferences."

Ms. Tenreyro was awarded her Ph.D. from Harvard University and worked as an economist for the Federal Reserve Bank of Boston between 2002 and 2004.

Nomura analyst Jordan Rochester said her appointment suggested the BOE would be less inclined to raise rates than previously.

"Relative to the hawkish person she is replacing...and the view that new members do not usually jump straight into a bias, we would assume a slight swing to a more dovish vote," he said.

ECB Criticized for Missing Signals of Banco Popular's Troubles

By Todd Buell

BRUSSELSThe European Central Bank's top bank supervisor on Monday faced a grilling from European lawmakers, who accused the central bank of missing signs that Spanish lender Banco Popular was struggling before it declared the institution likely to fail earlier this month.

The ECB's declaration triggered the sale of Banco Popular to its larger Spanish rival Banco Santander for the nominal sum of 1.

Members of the European Parliament's monetary affairs committee criticized ECB supervisory decisions and pushed for information that at times Ms. Nouy couldn't provide. On some occasions, Ms. Nouy said she couldn't answer specific questions due to confidentiality restrictions.

The committee's chairman, Italian Roberto Gualtieri, said he was troubled that Banco Popular was "declared fine for a long time," only to end up needing a rescue.

Ms. Nouy said a post mortem would be conducted, but that she believed her institution acted appropriately.

"I fail to [see] even with the knowledge that we have today, what we could have done differently," she said. "I would say we have passed the test of the framework."

The comments come about two weeks after the ECB, which supervises the eurozone's largest banks, declared Banco Popular to be "failing or likely to fail." The takeover was the first test case for new European rules for resolving ailing banks without making taxpayers foot the bill.

Ms. Nouy urged lawmakers to give supervisory authorities greater power to intervene early if they felt that banks were headed in the wrong direction. She said authorities should be able to give a "strong warning to the bankers that you are taking a road that will [lead] you to resolution or liquidation if you do not make an immediate U turn."

In introductory remarks Monday, Ms. Nouy expressed concern that financial firms moving to the Continent due to Brexit could exploit gaps in financial regulation there to avoid oversight.

She said the supervisor was ensuring that banks moving to the Continent from the U.K. are treated in the same way regardless of the location they choose, "and that there is no leeway for arbitrage between supervisory approaches."

She said, though, that there were limits to what the ECB could do "and we are concerned about that." She said that so-called options and discretions, or areas where national regulators maintain autonomy, "may now have a substantial impact in the context of Brexit."

She said that banks coming to the eurozone "may exploit supervisory loopholes by carrying out banklike activities through an investment firm or third-country branches, both of which are not supervised at euro area level, but rather at national level."

She added that it was "undesirable" that the European Union's "fragmented regulatory and supervisory framework" could play a role in a bank's decision-making process with respect to relocation.

South Africa Central Bank Rejects Watchdog Demand to Change Mandate

By Gabriele Steinhauser

JOHANNESBURGThe South African Reserve Bank on Tuesday rejected a demand by the head of the country's corruption watchdog to shift its mandate away from focusing on price stability, saying the request falls outside her remit.

Public Protector Busisiwe Mkhwebane on Monday said that South Africa's constitution should be adapted to ensure that the central bank focuses on protecting "the socio-economic well-being of the citizens" rather than the "the value of the currency."

She also said the bank should be required to regularly consult with parliament "to achieve meaningful socio-economic transformation." At the moment, that part of the constitution just required regular consultations with cabinet without the objective of changing the distribution of wealth.

More than two decades after the end of apartheid, South Africa's economy remains one of the world's most unequal, with much of the country's wealth and land owned by whites.

Ms. Mkhwebane's demand for constitutional change sent the rand currency lower and raised concern among analysts and the bank itself.

"The amendment would, if effected, strip the Reserve Bank of its key competency to protect the value of the currency and the well understood role that central banks play in securing price stability" and undermine the bank's independence, the SARB said in a statement Tuesday.

It said that the demand for changing the bank's constitutional mandate fell outside the public protector's powers and that it was ready to challenge it in court.

Ms. Mkhwebane argued that the constitutional change was a necessary remedial action after finding that the SARB failed to recover money spent on a series of apartheid-era bailouts for Bankcorp, now part of Barclay PLC's African unit Absa. She ordered Absa repay 1.125 billion rand, a finding that Absa rejected.

Analysts said that changes to the constitution were unlikely in the near term, since they require a 2/3 majority in parliament.

However, the comments form part of broader criticism of the SARB's policies, which have kept the bank's main repo rate at a high 7% for much of the past year in an effort to bring down inflation.

The powerful Congress of South African Trade Unions, which is a key ally of the ruling African National Congress, has long called on the bank to move away from inflation-targeting and focus more on economic growth and job creation.

Uganda Central Bank Cuts Main Lending Rate

By Nicholas Bariyo

KAMPALA UgandaUganda's central bank cut its key lending rate on Monday for the eighth consecutive time in a bid to boost sluggish economic growth.

The interest rate was slashed to 10% from 11% in Uganda, which is one of Africa's largest coffee exports.

"Given the continued weak growth prospects, Bank of Uganda judges that continued easing monetary policy is appropriate," Central Bank Governor Emmanuel Tumusiime-Mutebile said Monday.

Annual gross domestic product growth slowed to 3.9% in the 2016-2017 fiscal year, according to the Uganda Bureau of Statistics, coming in below expectations of around 5.5% growth, and down from 4.7% in 2015-2016.

Agricultural production was curtailed by drought and private-sector credit growth was slack.

The central bank's easing cycle started in April 2016 and the latest cut was in line with most analysts' expectationsdespite a recent spike in inflation.

Steep food prices drove inflation in May to 7.2%the highest level since January 2016from 6.8% in April.

"With the balance of risks to growth not improving so much, the easing mode is likely to continue," said Stephen Kaboyo, managing director with Ugandan fund manager Alpha Capital Partners.

The International Monetary Fund expects Uganda's economic growth to rebound and reach 5.5% in 2017-18, driven by higher government spending on infrastructure. The country is expected to hike overall public spending by 10% in the fiscal year starting July.

The East African country hopes its oil discoveries will also lift future economic growth. It is investing in electricity plants, railways and an oil pipeline ahead of planned oil production in 2020.

Exports of coffeeUganda's main exporting commodityare currently rebounding, after falling by 4% to 3.32 million bags in the 2015-2016 seasonthe third consecutive season of decline. Shipments rose by 29% in the first seven months of the 2016-17 season, boosted by maturing plantations.

New Evidence for a Lower New Normal in Interest Rates

Researchers have long been puzzling over the causes behind the decline in interest rates since 2000, and new evidence from financial markets suggests a decline in the natural rate of interest [http://www.frbsf.org/economic-research/publications/economic-letter/2017/june/financial-market-evidence-for-lower-natural-interest-rate-r-star/?utm_source=mailchimp&utm_medium=email&utm_campaign=economic-letter-2017-06-19] has contributed about 2 percentage points to the general downward trend in yields over the past two decades. Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco researchers Jens H.E. Christensen and Glenn D. Rudebusch estimate the equilibrium real rate using only prices of inflation-indexed bonds, a financial measure that differs from estimates drawn using macroeconomic models. The issue of whether there has been a shift in the equilibrium interest rate is quite important for investors, policy makers and researchers, the authors note.

Markets Must Grasp that the Fed is No Longer their Best Friend

Markets are failing to internalize the shift in the Fed's policy regime to normalize its interest rate [https://www.ft.com/content/797a800a-5278-11e7-a1f2-db19572361bb] structure and balance sheet, writes Mohamed El-Erian in the Financial Times. "As such, it is more willing to look through' weak growth and inflation data.," he said. Traders and investors, though, seem to be resisting the Fed's forward guidance, instead "betting on the repeat of the past few years during which officials have tended to over-estimate the extent of policy tightening." It is likely that "the central bank is now focusing more on excessive risk-taking by investors and traders, including its potential negative impact on the economy down the road."

The Fed's Poor Record on Soft Landings

The Wall Street Journal's Justin Lahart notes two things investors should keep in mind about the Federal Reserve: "One, it is in a tightening cycle [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/the-feds-poor-record-on-soft-landings-1497890842]. Two, tightening cycles almost always end badly." The aim of the Fed's tightening policy "is to guide the economy to a soft landing," Mr. Lahart writes. "But executing a soft landing is notoriously difficult to pull off. The Fed can only guess at what the economy's just-right levels of growth and employment are, and at what level of interest rates is consistent with hitting those marks."

Economy
Fed's Fischer Expresses Caution About Global Housing Markets; Fed vice chairman says rising home prices could be a result of extended periods of low interest rates'
By Harriet Torry
371 words
20 June 2017
03:15
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright 2017, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Federal Reserve Vice Chairman Stanley Fischer urged global policy makers to continue efforts to strengthen the resilience of their housing markets, echoing earlier cautionary words from Fed officials about the potential risks of having low interest rates over long periods.

"House prices are now high and rising in several countries, perhaps as a result of extended periods of low interest rates," Mr. Fischer said in prepared remarks released ahead of delivery Tuesday at a closed-door conference on macroprudential policy hosted by the Dutch and Swedish central banks in Amsterdam.

While Mr. Fischer said the financial system is stronger than before the recession that ended in 2009, "there is more to be done, and much improvement to be preserved and built on, for the world as we know it cannot afford another pair of crises of the magnitude of the Great Recession and the global financial crisis."

The Fed's No. 2 official highlighted the need for a well-capitalized banking system in promoting financial stability, as well as stress tests that examine scenarios with sharp declines in house prices. Mr. Fischer didn't comment on the immediate outlook for U.S. monetary policy in his comments.

Other Fed officials have spoken out about the threat of asset price bubbles emerging due to years of low interest rates. Boston Fed chief Eric Rosengren has long worried about potential bubbles in the hot commercial real-estate market. Minneapolis Fed President Neel Kashkari said in May that taking steps to make the financial sector more resilient is perhaps the best way to deal with asset price bubbles.

Mr. Fischer said macroprudential policies that focus on borrowers, lenders and securitization may be useful in strengthening financial stability, but "even though macroprudential policies reduce the incidence and severity of housing related crises, they may still occur."

He also said that "a well-capitalized banking system is a necessary condition for stability in bank-based financial systems as well as those with large nonbank sectors."

Fleshing Out the Trump FinReg Agenda

This week could offer an early glimpse at how the Trump administration might act on changes to financial regulation outlined in the recent Treasury Department report. [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/trump-administration-proposes-wide-ranging-changes-to-financial-sector-regulations-1497304932]

On Thursday, Keith Noreika, the acting comptroller of the currency, will be the first sitting Trump regulator to testify before a congressional panel [https://www.banking.senate.gov/public/index.cfm/hearings?ID=BC3D7D11-58DC-494C-A3B2-249267B45469] on steps policy makers can take to ease rules and foster economic growth. Mr. Noreika will appear on a panel alongside Federal Reserve governor Jerome Powell and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp . head Martin Gruenberg , as well as two other banking regulators.

Mr. Noreika and Mr. Powell, who is temporarily serving as the Fed's point person on financial regulation, are expected to outline their respective agencies' approaches to regulation over the coming months and years. Both have publicly backed taking a second look at postcrisis regulations to consider rolling back measures that haven't worked as intended. A key example is the Volcker rule that aims to prevent banks from making risky bets with their own money. Messrs. Noreika and Powell have expressed support for easing the rule, including by clarifying which trading activities are permitted under it.

"Sometimes regulators need to step back and take stock and say: Well, gee, what works and what was piled on?' " Mr. Noreika said in a May interview [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/the-volcker-rule-how-trumps-new-regulator-may-unleash-big-banks-1494495001] with The Wall Street Journal.

Thursday's hearing could give both policy makers an opportunity to outline how, exactly, they are considering refashioning the rule, as well as specific changes they hope to make to other postcrisis requirements, including those outlined in the Treasury report. Meanwhile, as we reported last week, the Fed is already laying the groundwork to ease certain bank capital requirements [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/fed-considers-easing-capital-rule-seen-as-hampering-swaps-market-1497432602] that industry officials say have hampered the swaps market.

The policy makers also are likely to field questions on round one of the Fed's annual "stress tests," set to be released hours after Thursday's hearing. The first part will show the Fed's assessment of the banking system as a whole. A second part, set for release next week, will show whether individual banks passed or failed.

The hearing also serves as a bellwether for assessing the likelihood that Senate lawmakers will come to any legislative agreement to roll back the 2010 Dodd-Frank regulatory-overhaul law.

Senate Banking Committee Chairman Mike Crapo (R., Idaho) has said he is working with Ohio Sen. Sherrod Brown [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/trump-financial-blueprint-will-help-senators-craft-regulatory-rollback-lawmaker-says-1497376722], the panel's top Democrat, on a bill that would ease requirements of Dodd-Frank and other industry regulations. Both say they are optimistic about striking a deal but haven't announced any specifics.

Republicans and Democrats in the Senate so far have been able to agree only on relatively minor changes to Dodd-Frank. Thursday's hearing could provide a progress report on their behind-the-scenes efforts.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro: Fed's Fischer Expresses Caution About Global Housing Markets

By Harriet Torry

Federal Reserve Vice Chairman Stanley Fischer urged global policy makers to continue efforts to strengthen the resilience of their housing markets, echoing earlier cautionary words from Fed officials about the potential risks of having low interest rates over long periods.

"House prices are now high and rising in several countries, perhaps as a result of extended periods of low interest rates," Mr. Fischer said in prepared remarks released ahead of delivery Tuesday at a closed-door conference on macroprudential policy hosted by the Dutch and Swedish central banks in Amsterdam.

While Mr. Fischer said the financial system is stronger than before the recession that ended in 2009, "there is more to be done, and much improvement to be preserved and built on, for the world as we know it cannot afford another pair of crises of the magnitude of the Great Recession and the global financial crisis."

The Fed's No. 2 official highlighted the need for a well-capitalized banking system in promoting financial stability, as well as stress tests that examine scenarios with sharp declines in house prices. Mr. Fischer didn't comment on the immediate outlook for U.S. monetary policy in his comments.

Other Fed officials have spoken out about the threat of asset price bubbles emerging due to years of low interest rates. Boston Fed chief Eric Rosengren has long worried about potential bubbles in the hot commercial real-estate market. Minneapolis Fed President Neel Kashkari said in May that taking steps to make the financial sector more resilient is perhaps the best way to deal with asset price bubbles.

Mr. Fischer said macroprudential policies that focus on borrowers, lenders and securitization may be useful in strengthening financial stability, but "even though macroprudential policies reduce the incidence and severity of housing related crises, they may still occur."

He also said that "a well-capitalized banking system is a necessary condition for stability in bank-based financial systems as well as those with large nonbank sectors."

WSJ Pro: ECB Criticized for Missing Signals of Banco Popular's Troubles

By Todd Buell

BRUSSELSThe European Central Bank 's top bank supervisor on Monday faced a grilling from European lawmakers, who accused the central bank of missing signs that Spanish lender Banco Popular was struggling before it declared the institution likely to fail earlier this month.

The ECB's declaration triggered the sale of Banco Popular to its larger Spanish rival Banco Santander for the nominal sum of 1.

Members of the European Parliament 's monetary affairs committee criticized ECB supervisory decisions and pushed for information that at times Ms. Nouy couldn't provide. On some occasions, Ms. Nouy said she couldn't answer specific questions due to confidentiality restrictions.

The committee's chairman, Italian Roberto Gualtieri, said he was troubled that Banco Popular was "declared fine for a long time," only to end up needing a rescue.

Ms. Nouy said a post mortem would be conducted, but that she believed her institution acted appropriately.

"I fail to [see] even with the knowledge that we have today, what we could have done differently," she said. "I would say we have passed the test of the framework."

The comments come about two weeks after the ECB, which supervises the eurozone's largest banks, declared Banco Popular to be "failing or likely to fail." The takeover was the first test case for new European rules for resolving ailing banks without making taxpayers foot the bill.

Ms. Nouy urged lawmakers to give supervisory authorities greater power to intervene early if they felt that banks were headed in the wrong direction. She said authorities should be able to give a "strong warning to the bankers that you are taking a road that will [lead] you to resolution or liquidation if you do not make an immediate U turn."

In introductory remarks Monday, Ms. Nouy expressed concern that financial firms moving to the Continent due to Brexit could exploit gaps in financial regulation there to avoid oversight.

She said the supervisor was ensuring that banks moving to the Continent from the U.K. are treated in the same way regardless of the location they choose, "and that there is no leeway for arbitrage between supervisory approaches."

She said, though, that there were limits to what the ECB could do "and we are concerned about that." She said that so-called options and discretions, or areas where national regulators maintain autonomy, "may now have a substantial impact in the context of Brexit."

She said that banks coming to the eurozone "may exploit supervisory loopholes by carrying out banklike activities through an investment firm or third-country branches, both of which are not supervised at euro area level, but rather at national level."

She added that it was "undesirable" that the European Union 's "fragmented regulatory and supervisory framework" could play a role in a bank's decision-making process with respect to relocation.

Barclays Charged In Qatari Case

By Max Colchester and Margot Patrick

The U.K.'s Serious Fraud Office on Tuesday filed criminal charges against Barclays PLC and four former top executives linked to their handling of Middle Eastern investments that rescued the bank at the height of the financial crisis.

The case marks the first time that top executives at a U.K. bank face criminal charges for their actions during the financial crisis. If Barclays is found guilty it faces a fine but wouldn't lose its banking license.

The SFO charged the individuals and the bank with conspiracy to commit fraud. Two individuals, including its former Chief Executive John Varley , were also charged with the provision of unlawful financial assistance.

The allegations center on how Barclays structured two capital injections from Qatari investors as the bank raised 11.8 billion ($15 billion) to prop it up during the 2008 financial market meltdown. Barclays said it paid 322 million in "advisory services" to Qatari investors, which wasn't initially disclosed after the capital was raised.

The SFO's charges also relate to a $3 billion loan facility Barclays made to the State of Qatar acting through the ministry of economy and finance in November 2008, just after its second capital raise.

Barclays said in a statement it is "considering its position in relation to these developments."

Unlike its peers Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC and Lloyds Banking Group PLC , the Middle Eastern equity injections meant Barclays was able to avoid a bailout by British taxpayers.

The SFO's case involves a cadre of Barclays top executives who steered the bank at the time. Mr. Varley, who was the bank's chief executive until 2011 and Roger Jenkins, a former top investment bank executive who played a key role in orchestrating the capital raises, were charged with conspiracy to commit fraud and unlawful financial assistance. Thomas Kalaris, who used to run the bank's wealth division, and Richard Boath, who headed the bank's European financial institutions group, were charged with conspiracy to commit fraud.

A law firm representing Mr. Varley said it couldn't immediately comment. Mr. Jenkins intends to vigorously defend against the charges, his lawyer, Brad Kaufman at Greenberg Traurig said, adding Mr. Jenkins had received internal and external legal advice on all the matters covered in the SFO case. A spokesman for Mr. Boath said he didn't immediately have comment. Mr. Kalaris hung up the phone when asked for comment.

Under U.K. law, when the men appear at the magistrate's court, they will have the option to enter a plea. Any bail conditions will be set and prosecutors will give a brief synopsis of their case. Magistrates then decide which court will hear it. They will appear before a court in London on July 3.

The Barclays case is a major test for the SFO, which has in recent years failed to successfully prosecute a number of high profile bribery and corruption cases. U.K. Prime Minister Theresa May recently pledged to fold the SFO into another crime fighting agency.

Barclays is already facing other legal problems linked to the Qatar capital raisings. In a separate probe, the U.K.'s Financial Conduct Authority imposed a 50 million fine over the disclosure failures. The FCA said earlier this year that it was reviewing the case after being handed new evidence. Barclays is contesting the fine.

The U.S. Justice Department and the Securities and Exchange Commission are also investigating the payments made to Middle Eastern officials. Separately PCP Capital Partners, a private-equity group, is suing Barclays for $1 billion alleging it made "sham payments" to the Middle Eastern investors. PCP, which helped organize Abu Dhabi's investment in Barclays in 2008, also alleges that Barclays lent Qatar investors $3 billion to invest back into the bank. Barclays has previously denied this.

The court case is another distraction for Barclays 's current chief executive, Jes Staley , as he attempts to clean up the bank. Mr. Staley is himself currently under investigation by U.K. regulators for trying to identify a whistleblower at the bank.

Sen. Warren Calls for Removal of 12 Wells Fargo Directors

By Emily Glazer

Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D, Mass.) is urging the Federal Reserve to remove a dozen Wells Fargo board directors who served during the bank's sales-practices scandal, according to a letter reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

In the letter sent to Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen on Monday, Sen. Warren urged the Fed to invoke its authority under a rule that allows it to remove certain people associated with depository institutions under specific circumstances.

Sen. Warren pointed to 12 directors who served on the San Francisco bank's board between May 2011 and July 2015, a period in which Wells Fargo fired 5,300 employees for the bad behavior. The bank settled with two regulators and a city official in September for $185 million over the practices. Its chief executive later abruptly retired and the bank continues to face state and federal investigations, which it has said it is cooperating with.

A Wells Fargo spokeswoman said the board and its management team have "taken many actions in response to its retail sales practices issues, including changes in senior leadership, executive accountability actions and numerous steps to ensure we make things right with any customer affected by unacceptable sales practices." She added that the work is ongoing.

The bank's board has also endured harsh criticism and faced opposition during a contentious shareholder meeting in late April. Several directors were re-elected to their roles by just a slim margin.

Sen. Warren has been a sharp critic of Wells Fargo 's management and handling of the sales-practices scandal. When former CEO John Stumpf appeared before the Senate Banking Committee to explain the problems, Sen. Warren lambasted him and said he should resign and be criminally investigated.

In her eight-page letter to the Fed, Sen. Warren wrote that the sales-practices scandal "revealed severe problems with the bank's risk management practicesproblems that justify the Federal Reserve's removal of all responsible Board members."

In the wake of the scandal, Wells Fargo 's board conducted an investigation of the bank, but largely didn't fault directors. On a call with media after the report was disclosed in April, Chairman Stephen Sanger said "the findings of the investigation showed the board took the appropriate actions with the information it had, when it had it" and that it hired a law firm to do the investigation to get "an independent and objective assessment of our performance."

Sen. Warren's letter cited the Fed's ability to remove board members including if they "engaged or participated in any unsafe or unsound practice" that caused certain depository institutions to "suffer financial loss" and that demonstrated "continuing disregardfor the safety and soundness" of the institution.

"I urge the Federal Reserve to use the tools Congress has given it to remove the responsible Board members and protect the continued safety and soundness of one of the country's largest banks," the senator's letter said.

The 12 board directors mentioned in the letter are: John D. Baker II , John S. Chen , Lloyd H. Dean , Elizabeth A. Duke , Enrique Hernandez, Jr ., Donald M. James , Cynthia H. Milligan , Federico F. Pena , James H. Quigley , Stephen W. Sanger , Susan G. Swenson , and Suzanne M. Vautrinot.

Among those directors, Ms. Duke, who is now the board's vice chair, is a former Fed governor.

Sen. Warren wrote to the Fed that the board "failed to create an adequate risk management framework that would have alerted it to systemic problems with retail sales practices. It also caused long-lasting reputational damage to the bank that has eroded the bank's customer base."

The board's own report about the sales scandal, according to the letter, showed its "continuing disregard" for the bank's safety and soundness and failure to seriously address the sales-practices issues despite years of concerns.

The board's April report largely placed blame on Mr. Stumpf and former retail-banking head Carrie Tolstedt . The board clawed back an additional $75 million in compensation from them both.

The company was slow to stamp out the issues, the report said, in part because its decentralized structure gave Ms. Tolstedt too much power and officials from other units, such as risk management, human resources and legal, either couldn't connect the dots on problems or weren't powerful enough to do anything about it.

In late 2015, board members, including then-lead independent director Mr. Sanger, pushed Mr. Stumpf to remove Ms. Tolstedt from her role. But Mr. Stumpf declined, saying that Ms. Tolstedt, while at times controlling, was "the best banker in America," the report said.

The report also highlighted how the bank's push to boost revenue and profit trickled down to thousands of employees who felt pressured to meet unrealistic sales goals.

Citigroup 's Plan to Revive Its Lagging Stock

By Telis Demos

Investors want Michael Corbat to change the narrative at Citigroup Inc .

Since taking the bank's helm in October 2012, Mr. Corbat's Citigroup has played defense. It has scaled back businesses, shrunk the firm's global retail-banking footprint and wound down crisis-era assets that dragged on returns.

For shareholders, it has been a slog. Citigroup 's total return has underperformed those of its biggest rivals, save for Wells Fargo & Co ., since Mr. Corbat arrived on the scene. The bank has yet to show it can consistently generate returns that exceed its cost of capital; in the first quarter the bank's return on common equity was a tepid 7.4%. And the stock trades at just 85% of book value, the lowest valuation of the big U.S. banks.

Now, investors, analysts and even top executives within the bank say it is time for Mr. Corbat, a former Harvard football star, to shift to offense. That means boosting growth and profitability in areas such as credit cards, Wall Street stock trading and retail banking.

"What investors want now is to understand what's next in the story," said Conor Muldoon, fundamental portfolio manager at Causeway Capital Management LLC. "What can you do to make the valuation better over time?"

Citigroup is among the largest holdings in the firm's Global Value fund.

The first test comes this week and next with the Federal Reserve's annual bank stress tests. A key indicator will be the bank's so-called payout ratio under the tests, a measure of whether it is given a green light to return more capital to shareholders than it is expected to earn in coming quarters.

Citigroup shareholders want to see the payout ratio top 100%, meaning the bank would begin drawing down its capital pile rather than continue adding to it. If it can't do this, gains in its business won't accrue to shareholders.

Assuming that won't be the case, investors want Mr. Corbat to boost returns over the long term by generating more profit on lagging businesses, notably the bank's consumer-lending and retail-banking arm.

In anticipation of this, Mr. Corbat has been honing the bank's focus, a notable change for a firm that was once a sprawling global behemoth. He and his veteran management team have done things like exit nearly two dozen retail markets globally and slash the number of corporate clients to 14,000 from 30,000.

The idea is to emphasize areas with the strongest growth prospects, particularly ones in which Citigroup has a dominant share with multinational consumers and corporations. In this vein, the bank has already said it would invest a further $1 billion to upgrade its Mexican retail bank.

The biggest recent bet, though, is on credit cards, particularly for U.S. shoppers. Over the past two years, Citigroup has taken on more than $10 billion in card loans for Costco Wholesale Corp ., and made other investments, such as in Citigroup 's proprietary reward-card offerings.

As a result, Citigroup has the biggest global card lending portfolio of any U.S. bank. Card lending now makes up 24% of Citigroup 's total loans, nearly double that of rivals such as Bank of America Corp . and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., according to analysts at Sanford Bernstein.

Underscoring the importance of cards and the Costco business, which the bank took over from American Express Corp ., Mr. Corbat personally made surprise trips in December to call centers in Florence, Ky., Jacksonville, Fla., and Tucson, Ariz., according to people familiar with the trips.

A key point was to emphasize how critical customer service will be to get new Costco -card customers, who in many cases were offered introductory rates that would expire, to become loyal users of their cards.

Those rates and other investment in cards were a drag on the bank's retail performance of late. While corporate and investment banking was at or near return targets last year, consumer banking generated a roughly 13% return against a 20% target, Mr. Corbat said recently.

Citigroup is counting on the profitability of the card business to pick up meaningfully in the second half of this year as the initial investment period winds down.

Mr. Corbat and other executives are expected to provide greater detail for these plans when the bank in July holds an investor dayits first in nearly a decade. At this, he also will have to fight the view among some investors and analysts that Citigroup lacks a unifying strategy.

The bank's consumer and corporate businesses are like "two boats floating in the ocean, not tied to each other," said KBW analyst Brian Kleinhanzl. In the past, he has called for the bank's breakup, though Mr. Kleinhanzl says he is now waiting to see how Citigroup fares amid rising interest rates.

The investor day will give Mr. Corbat "the chance to lay out the new strategy," he added.

FTSE Russell Index Considers Booting Firms With Lots of Non-Voting Shares

By Richard Teitelbaum

A proposal being floated by a large index firm could force finance chiefs at companies like Alphabet Inc ., Facebook Inc . and Ford Motor Co . to choose between keeping their places in broad stock benchmarks or changing their share class structures.

FTSE Russell is proposing possible restrictions on the inclusion of companies with unequal voting rights in its indexes, but the firm will weigh input from clients and investors before working out specifics.

The proposal calls for setting a minimum threshold for the percentage of voting control attached to company shares in an index. For example, a company whose Class A shares in an index control 40% of the total votes might be excluded from FTSE Russell's main indexes, like the Russell 3000 or Russell 2000, if the threshold were higher than that.

FTSE Russell gave until Friday to respond to its proposal. A spokesman said it expects to release a decision sometime in July.

The firm asked for feedback on what the threshold might be. It also said it might ultimately decide not to change FTSE Russell rules at all.

Nevertheless, the proposal is addressing a corporate governance issue viewed as important to some key investors. "The future of the markets are at stake," said James Andrus, an investment manager at the California Public Employees' Retirement System .

Many institutional investors have grown increasingly critical of unequal voting rights. For example, organizations including the Council of Institutional Investors, an advocacy group, have called for nonvoting shares to be banned from indexes.

Whether barring companies with unequal voting rights from indexes would prompt them to opt for a different share class structure when going public is uncertain. "The answer would depend on how restrictive [the ban is], that is how many indexes we were excluded from," said Fitbit Inc. Chief Financial Officer Bill Zerella in an email. "We would have to weigh that with the benefits of a dual class."

Fitbit went public in 2015 with Class A shares entitled to one vote and Class B shares entitled to 10 votes.

All things being equal, companies that are shifted from popular indexes are likely to see their share prices fall because investors who track the benchmark are no longer required to hold the stock in question. "There are some studies that show it could make a 15% difference," Mr. Andrus said.

FTSE Russell and two rivals began considering the issue of nonvoting share classes after the March initial public offering of Snap Inc ., whose Class A shares carry no voting rights. The new FTSE Russell proposal broadens that to other kinds of unequal voting shares.

And the fact that FTSE Russell described a system using thresholds to limit the inclusion of shares with unequal voting rights suggests the firm is likely to do something.

"I do think there's a high likelihood that one of these thresholds will be implemented," said Aeisha Mastagni, a portfolio manager at the California State Teachers' Retirement System . "The question is what the threshold will be."

FTSE Russell estimated the percentage of voting power held by the public of selected companies that have shares with unequal voting rights. By the firm's estimates, 38.64% of Alphabet's voting power is represented by the shares included in its main indexes.

For Facebook , 30.27% of total votes are represented by the shares in its main indexes and 59.64% for Ford Motor .

Alphabet and Facebook didn't respond to emails seeking comment on the proposal or FTSE Russell's estimates.

A Ford spokesman referred to a section of the company's 2017 proxy statement that says, "Our ownership structure has helped insulate our Company from business cycles and related short-term pressures."

Critics of unequal voting rights are likely to applaud restrictions. "The mere fact that they are considering keeping nonvoting shares out of the index is a good thing," said Charles Elson, a professor of corporate governance at the University of Delaware.

Kathleen Smith, co-founder of investment firm Renaissance Capital LLC, said excluding dual voting class companies may result in leaving out superior stock-market performers. "This could be a minefield," she said.

FTSE Russell, which is owned by London Stock Exchange Group PLC , solicited suggestions on what kinds of restrictions should be applied to companies that fail to meet the threshold. All of a company's securities might be excluded or just nonvoting shares, for example.

The firm also sought input on how new restrictions should be applied. The thresholds could be applied at the next review, for example, or existing constituents could be grandfathered into the indexes.

Rival index firms S&P Dow Jones Indices and MSCI Inc . also have launched reviews concerning the inclusion of companies with nonvoting shares in their indexes. An S&P spokeswoman said the firm would complete its consultations with market participants on June 30 and announce its decision soon after.

An MSCI spokeswoman didn't return requests for comment.

The Virtual Currency War That Threatens to Tear Bitcoin Apart

By Paul Vigna

A calcifying rift in the bitcoin world is pitting two camps with competing visions for what the virtual currency should be against each other, accentuating the volatility that has come to define this market.

Even for bitcoin, though, last week was a wild ride. It rose to a high of $3,018, only to fall 27%. The currency then recovered somewhat and by Tuesday was trading at $2,650.

The sharp swings followed bitter jousting by bitcoin developers and businesses over how best to expand the currency's processing capacity and could result in it being split into separate, competing coins. On one side: those who want bitcoin to act more like a digital version of gold, a commodity with a limited supply that can be held as a store of value.

The opposing camp wants bitcoin to look more like a currency, a unit that can be used to settle transactions. That faction wants bitcoin to be as fast and easy to trade as a dollar.

The two sides have been at a stalemate, but new proposals coming this summer could provide a breakthrougheither changes in the bitcoin network's processing capacity or a split between a high-speed and slow-speed bitcoin. "You watch a fine marriage that is falling apart," says Emin Gun Sirer, a Cornell professor who studies virtual currencies. "Eventually, you say, I think a divorce is the best thing."

The bickering has hindered interest in the virtual currency, sending some to a newer alternative, Ethereum , that has surged more than fortyfold this year and is now worth only about 20% less than all the traded bitcoin, according to CoinMarketCap.com.

"A lot of my friends are selling their bitcoin and buying ethereum," says Zachary Mallard, who runs a men's grooming-products business in Brooklyn, N.Y. Mr. Mallard bought some ethereum in recent months, passing up on bitcoin in part because he views the potential split in the currency as limiting the potential gains.

The debate between the two sides has intensified in recent weeks, as bitcoin has rallied more than 160% this yearand that is despite the recent swings in the currency's value. Ultimately, how the trading is structured will help determine who controls the increasingly valuable currency, and who potentially gets rich from that control.

As it was developed in 2008 by the pseudonymous creator, Satoshi Nakamoto, bitcoin has a limited supply, 21 million coins, that are released on a set schedule and awarded to businesses that confirm transactions in a process known as "mining."

The network it trades on was designed to be fast and cheap, operating without middlemen, with an immutable ledger recording transactions. The ledger, also known as blockchain, was simultaneously transparent to users and protective of the identity of buyers and sellers, some of whom historically used bitcoin for money laundering or other illegal purposes.

Briefly, this is how bitcoin trades: individual transactions on the network are packed into blocks by companies that specialize in processing the trades, so-called "miners." Under its current structure, the size of those blocks is capped, allowing only about seven transactions per second, far below the hundreds of thousands a network like Visa can process.

In bitcoin's early days, the limit wasn't an issue. That has changed as bitcoin transactions have surged and the currency has piqued more widespread curiosity. The result has been bottlenecks and rising transaction fees.

Many investors and processors have pushed to increase the limits in an effort to make bitcoin trade more like a modern-day currency. But purists and some developers have fought back, in part because it could hurt their financial interests but also because it goes against their libertarian, anti-big business bent.

With past efforts at compromise between the two sides failing, "we've reached the point now where we accept that it's a possibility" that the factions will go their separate ways, said Eric Lombrozo, one of bitcoin's leading developers.

Last week, a prominent bitcoin mining company, BitMain, released a plan to alter bitcoin's underlying software that would result in two live versions at the same time. There are other proposals as well that would have the same effect. It isn't clear which if any will be adopted.

On paper, everybody seems to be in favor of raising the capacity limits. In practice, there are two sides starkly divided over how to do that. One group, led by businesses, wants to raise the size limit for all bitcoin trading. The other, led by developers, wants to create what is essentially another, subordinate network for processing transactions quickly. This option would leave the current main network unchanged.

People involved in the fight say the fate of bitcoin may become clearer in the next two months as owners and miners vote on the various proposals to break the logjam.

Arching over all of this is the question of who ultimately controls bitcoin. "Why is it so acrimonious?" Mr. Gun Sirer asked. "It isn't about the parameters, it's not about the block size. This is about voice."

Aaron Lucchetti contributed this article.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

Road to FinReg Reform Largely Avoids Congress

Of the 97 proposed changes including in Treasury's financial regulation white paper last week, 62 can be enacted simply through rulemaking, Goldman Sachs analysts say. And with 19 of 22 key regulatory leadership positions opening up over the next 18 months, the administration will get a chance to prove its "personnel is policy" line. The areas most dependent on congressional action are changes to the Volcker ban against proprietary trading and private-fund sponsorship, as well as any dismantling or reorganization of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau . (liz.hoffman@wsj.com; @lizrhoffman)

Morgan Stanley 's FIG Group Keeps Thinning Out

Deutsche Bank (DBK.XE) is hiring a Morgan Stanley managing director, Meir Lewis, to run its insurance coverage investment-banking group. It's the latest managing-director-level departure from MS's financial-institutions group, following Eric Bischof to Bank of America (BAC) and Christian Lown to Navient (NAVI). FIG is a bit of a prestige group at MS; it played a key role advising the Treasury Department during the financial crisis, and has produced the bank's two most recent CFOs. (liz.hoffman@wsj.com; @lizrhoffman)

Canada Edges Closer to Bail-in Bank Regime

Canada is putting the final touches on a process to design a bail-in regime for Canada's big banks to reduce the risk for taxpayers should a lender get in trouble. Finance Department released proposals for consideration, and stakeholders can respond until July 17. Key element is Canada proposes that tradeable or transferable senior unsecured debt issued after the implementation date and with an initial term greater than 400 days will be subject to bail-in regime. Under bail-in regime, authorities can convert some of a failing bank's debt into common shares in order to recapitalize the bank and help restore it to viability. BMO Capital says proposal is largely in line with expectations, and believes bail-in securities will end up being a bit more expensive than legacy senior unsecured securities. (paul.vieira@wsj.com; @paulvieira)

Retail Presence Shouldn't Prevent Italian Bank Bail-Ins

Large retail holdings of subordinated bank debt in Italy wouldn't be enough to prevent a bail-in of troubled banks' creditors, ABN Amro strategist Tom Kinmonth says. Nevertheless, Italian authorities could still try to compensate mom-and-pop' investors via exchanges for senior debt or through a compensation fund. Therefore, even though retail holdings couldn't prevent a bail-in, they could reduce its benefit to failing lenders, the strategist adds. The eurozone's most recent bail-in took place in Spain, where holders of junior Banco Popular debt were wiped out as part of the bank's rescue plan.(tasos.vossos@wsj.com, @tasosvos)

China Shares End Higher on Top Regulator's Remarks on Brokerages

Chinese shares closed higher Monday led by brokerages, home appliance makers and property developers on a confluence of positive news. The benchmark Shanghai Composite Index ended the day up 0.7% at 3144.37, while the smaller Shenzhen market gained 0.6%. Securities firms were among the biggest winners, after the country's top regulator said over the weekend that China will continue to encourage brokerages to push ahead with innovation. Liu Shiyu, chairman of the China Securities Regulatory Commission, vowed at a meeting to create "world-class investment banks with Chinese characteristics." Strong Chinese housing price data released in the morning and the Chinese central bank 's decision to continue injecting more cash into markets also lifted investor sentiment. (hong.shen@wsj.com)

Big Decision on China and Passive Investing

The MSCI index will decide Tuesday [https://www-wsj-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/your-etf-isnt-what-you-think-it-is-1497889558] whether to include Chinese domestic stocks in the benchmark emerging markets index, a decision that could lead to hundreds of billions of dollars eventually being invested there. Its latest plan is to dilute the weight of China's domestically listed A shares to cater to the needs of index trackers, which makes sense as an investing algorithm. But that supposed benchmark is neither a sensible measure of what's happening in emerging markets, because China's weight is too small, nor a useful tool for big investors who have secured preferential access to the market.

Other algorithms can follow different rules. Vanguard runs $70 billion in the biggest emerging markets exchange-traded funds following a FTSE index that includes almost 5% in Chinese A shares, 10 times what MSCI is proposing, based on quotas allocated to large foreign investors. They may be simpler than the usual computer-driven trader, but the big decisions still matter: Vanguard's EM ETF is up 14.1% this year against 17.7% for Blackrock's iShares MSCI-driven ETF.

The Commodity Futures Trading Commission 's Market Risk Advisory Committee holds a public meeting [http://www.cftc.gov/idc/groups/public/@newsroom/documents/generic/mrac_agenda062017.pdf] to respond to a presentation on risk surveillance of central counterparties (CCPs), discuss how to better inform the CCP regulatory framework, and advise the commission of the potential effects of Brexit.
